<p><b>Mass surveillance</b> is the intricate <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">surveillance</a> of an entire or a substantial fraction of a population in order to monitor that group of citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The surveillance is often carried out by governments or <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_agency" title="Intelligence agency">governmental organisations</a>, but may also be carried out by corporations, either on behalf of governments or at their own initiative. Depending on each nation's laws and judicial systems, the legality of and the permission required to engage in mass surveillance varies. It is often distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Targeted_surveillance" title="Targeted surveillance">targeted surveillance</a>.</p>
<p>Mass surveillance has often been cited as necessary to fight <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a>, to prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_unrest" title="Social unrest">social unrest</a>, to protect <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a>, to fight <a href="/wiki/Child_pornography" title="Child pornography">child pornography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_protection" title="Child protection">protect children</a>. Conversely, mass surveillance has equally often been criticized for violating <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a> rights, limiting <a href="/wiki/Civil_and_political_rights" title="Civil and political rights">civil and political rights and freedoms</a>, and being illegal under some legal or constitutional systems. Another criticism is that increasing mass surveillance could lead to the development of a <a href="#Surveillance_state">surveillance state</a> or an <a href="#Electronic_police_state">electronic police state</a> where civil liberties are infringed or political dissent is undermined by <a href="/wiki/COINTELPRO" title="COINTELPRO">COINTELPRO</a>-like programs. Such a state could also be referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian state</a>.</p>
<p>In 2013, the practice of mass surveillance by world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> governments was called into question after <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>‘s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_Global_surveillance_disclosure" title="2013 Global surveillance disclosure">2013 global surveillance disclosure</a>. Reporting based on documents Snowden leaked to various media outlets triggered a debate about civil liberties and the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_privacy" title="Right to privacy">right to privacy</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_Age" title="Digital Age">Digital Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Mass surveillance is considered a <a href="/wiki/Global_issue" title="Global issue">global issue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: By country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Privacy_International" title="Privacy International">Privacy International</a>'s 2007 survey, covering 47 countries, indicated that there had been an increase in surveillance and a decline in the performance of privacy safeguards, compared to the previous year. Balancing these factors, eight countries were rated as being 'endemic surveillance societies'. Of these eight, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> scored lowest, followed jointly by <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, then jointly by <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. The best ranking was given to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, which was judged to have 'adequate safeguards against abuse'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many countries throughout the world have already been adding thousands of surveillance cameras to their urban, suburban and even rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yougov1_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-yougov1-10">[10]</a></sup> For example, in September 2007 the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_Liberties_Union" title="American Civil Liberties Union">American Civil Liberties Union</a> (ACLU) stated that we are "in danger of tipping into a genuine surveillance society completely alien to American values" with "the potential for a dark future where our every move, our every transaction, our every communication is recorded, compiled, and stored away, ready to be examined and used against us by the authorities whenever they want."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 12 March 2013 <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a> published a <i>Special report on Internet Surveillance</i>. The report included a list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in active, intrusive surveillance of news providers, resulting in grave violations of freedom of information and human rights. Five countries were placed on the initial list: <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_Australia" title="Mass surveillance in Australia">Mass surveillance in Australia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahrain">Bahrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bahrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> is one of the five countries on Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in active, intrusive surveillance of news providers, resulting in grave violations of freedom of information and human rights. The level of Internet filtering and surveillance in Bahrain is one of the highest in the world. The royal family is represented in all areas of Internet management and has sophisticated tools at its disposal for spying on its subjects. The online activities of dissidents and news providers are closely monitored and the surveillance is increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_China" title="Mass surveillance in China">Mass surveillance in China</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> is one of the five countries on Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in active, intrusive surveillance of news providers, resulting in grave violations of freedom of information and human rights. All Internet access in China is owned or controlled by the state or the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party</a>. Many foreign journalists in China have said that they take for granted that their telephones are tapped and their email is monitored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tools put in place to filter and monitor the Internet are collectively known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Firewall_of_China" title="Great Firewall of China">Great Firewall of China</a>. Besides the usual routing regulations that allow access to an IP address or a particular domain name to be blocked, the Great Firewall makes large-scale use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Packet_Inspection" title="Deep Packet Inspection">Deep Packet Inspection</a> (DPI) technology to monitor and block access based on keyword detection. The Great Firewall has the ability to dynamically block encrypted connections. One of the country’s main ISPs, <a href="/wiki/China_Unicom" title="China Unicom">China Unicom</a>, automatically cuts a connection as soon as it is used to transmit encrypted content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The monitoring system developed by China is not confined to the Great Firewall, monitoring is also built into social networks, chat services and VoIP. Private companies are directly responsible to the Chinese authorities for surveillance of their networks to ensure banned messages are not circulated. The QQ application, owned by the firm Tencent, allows the authorities to monitor in detail exchanges between Internet users by seeking certain keywords and expressions. The author of each message can be identified by his or her user number. The QQ application is effectively a giant Trojan horse. And since March 2012, new legislation requires all new users of micro-blogging sites to register using their own name and telephone number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">Skype</a>, one of the world’s most popular Internet telephone platforms, is closely monitored. Skype services in China are available through a local partner, the TOM media group. The Chinese-language version of Skype, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TOM-Skype" title="TOM-Skype">TOM-Skype</a>, is slightly different from the downloadable versions in other countries. A report by <a href="/wiki/OpenNet_Initiative" title="OpenNet Initiative">OpenNet Initiative Asia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> says everyday conversations are captured on servers. Interception and storage of a conversation may be triggered by a sender’s or recipient’s name or by keywords that occur in the conversation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 30 January, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> reported that it had been the target of attacks by the Chinese government. The first breach took place on 13 September 2012 when the newspaper was preparing to publish an article about the fortune amassed by the family of outgoing Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Wen_Jiabao" title="Wen Jiabao">Wen Jiabao</a>. The newspaper said the purpose of attacks was to identify the sources that supplied the newspaper with information about corruption among the prime minister’s entourage. The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a></i> also said they had been the targets of cyber attacks from China. In February, <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> disclosed that the accounts of some 250,000 subscribers had been the victims of attacks from China similar to those carried out on the <i>New York Times</i>. Mandiant, the company engaged by the NYT to secure its network, identified the source of the attacks as a group of hackers it called Advanced Persistent Threat 1, a unit of the People’s Liberation Army operating from a 12-storey building in the suburbs of Shanghai that had hundreds, possibly thousands, of staff and the direct support of the Chinese government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Germany">East Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: East Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_East_Germany" title="Mass surveillance in East Germany">Mass surveillance in East Germany</a></div>
<p>Before the Digital Revolution, one of the world's biggest mass surveillance operations was carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">secret police</a> of the former <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>. By the time the state collapsed in 1989, the Stasi had built up an estimated civilian network of 300,000 informants (approximately one in fifty of the population), who monitored even minute hints of political dissent among other citizens. Many <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germans</a> visiting friends and family in East Germany were also subject to Stasi spying, as well as many high-ranking West German politicians and persons in the public eye.</p>
<p>Most East German citizens were well aware that their government was spying on them, which led to a culture of mistrust: touchy political issues were only discussed in the comfort of their own four walls and only with the closest of friends and family members, while widely maintaining a façade of unquestioning followership in public.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The right to <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a> is a highly developed area of law in Europe. The <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Directive" title="Data Protection Directive">Data Protection Directive</a> regulates the processing of personal data within the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. For comparison, the US has no data protection law that is comparable to this; instead, the US regulates data protection on a sectoral basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since early 2012, the European Union has been working on a <a href="/wiki/General_Data_Protection_Regulation" title="General Data Protection Regulation">General Data Protection Regulation</a> to replace the <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Directive" title="Data Protection Directive">Data Protection Directive</a> and harmonise data protection and privacy law. On 20 October 2013, a committee at the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> backed the measure, which, if it is enacted, could require American companies to seek clearance from European officials before complying with United States warrants seeking private data. The vote is part of efforts in Europe to shield citizens from online surveillance in the wake of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_mass_surveillance_disclosures" title="2013 mass surveillance disclosures">revelations about a far-reaching spying program</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._National_Security_Agency" title="U.S. National Security Agency">U.S. National Security Agency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> European Union justice and rights commissioner Viviane Reding said "The question has arisen whether the large-scale collection and processing of personal information under US surveillance programmes is necessary and proportionate to meet the interests of national security." The EU is also asking the US for changes to US legislation to match the legal redress offered in Europe; American citizens in Europe can go to the courts if they feel their rights are infringed but Europeans without right of residence in America cannot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> When the EU / US arrangement to implement <a href="/wiki/International_Safe_Harbor_Privacy_Principles" title="International Safe Harbor Privacy Principles">International Safe Harbor Privacy Principles</a> were struck down by the European Court of Justice, a new framework for transatlantic data flows, called the "EU-US Privacy Shield", was adopted in July 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2014, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> declared invalid the EU <a href="/wiki/Data_Retention_Directive" title="Data Retention Directive">Data Retention Directive</a>. The Court said it violates two basic rights - respect for private life and protection of personal data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Union_legislative_procedure" title="European Union legislative procedure">legislative body</a> of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directive_2006/24/EC" title="Directive 2006/24/EC">Data Retention Directive</a> on 15 December 2005. It requires that telecommunication operators retain metadata for telephone, Internet, and other telecommunication services for periods of not less than six months and not more than two years from the date of the communication as determined by each EU member state and, upon request, to make the data available to various governmental bodies. Access to this information is not limited to investigation of serious crimes, nor is a warrant required for access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Undertaken under the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seventh_Framework_Programme" title="Seventh Framework Programme">Seventh Framework Programme</a></i> <i>for research and technological development</i> (FP7 - Science in Society<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>) some multidisciplinary and mission oriented mass surveillance activities (for example <a href="/wiki/INDECT" title="INDECT">INDECT</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://cordis.europa.eu/project/rcn/88614_en.html" rel="nofollow">HIDE</a>) were funded by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> in association with industrial partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikinews-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="759" height="22" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news: <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/Listening_to_you_at_last:_EU_plans_to_tap_cell_phones" title="wikinews:Listening to you at last: EU plans to tap cell phones">Listening to you at last: EU plans to tap cell phones</a></b></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The INDECT Project ("Intelligent information system supporting observation, searching and detection for security of citizens in urban environment")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INDECT_project_homepage_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-INDECT_project_homepage-29">[29]</a></sup> develops an intelligent urban environment observation system to register and exchange operational data for the automatic detection, recognition and intelligent processing of all information of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abnormal_behaviour" title="Abnormal behaviour">abnormal behaviour</a> or violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cordis.europa.eu_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-cordis.europa.eu-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph.co.uk_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph.co.uk-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main expected results of the INDECT project are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Trial of intelligent analysis of video and audio data for threat detection in urban environments,</li>
<li>Creation of tools and technology for privacy and data protection during storage and transmission of information using quantum cryptography and new methods of digital watermarking,</li>
<li>Performing computer-aided detection of threats and targeted crimes in Internet resources with privacy-protecting solutions,</li>
<li>Construction of a search engine for rapid semantic search based on watermarking of content related to child pornography and human organ trafficking,</li>
<li>Implementation of a distributed computer system that is capable of effective intelligent processing.</li>
</ul>
<p>HIDE ("Homeland Security, Biometric Identification &amp; Personal Detection Ethics")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> was a research project funded by the European Commission within the scope of the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Programmes_for_Research_and_Technological_Development" title="Framework Programmes for Research and Technological Development">Seventh RTD Framework Programme</a> (FP7). The consortium, coordinated by <a class="external text" href="http://www.emiliomordini.info" rel="nofollow">Emilio Mordini</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> explored the <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a> implications of <a href="/wiki/Biometrics" title="Biometrics">biometrics</a> and personal detection technologies, focusing on the continuum between personal detection, authentication, identification and mass surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2002 German citizens were tipped off about wiretapping, when a software error led to a phone number allocated to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=German_Secret_Service&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="German Secret Service (page does not exist)">German Secret Service</a> being listed on mobile telephone bills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_India" title="Mass surveillance in India">Mass surveillance in India</a></div>
<p>The Indian parliament passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_Technology_Act" title="Information Technology Act">Information Technology Act</a> of 2008 with no debate, giving the government fiat power to tap all communications without a court order or a warrant. Section 69 of the act states "Section 69 empowers the Central Government/State Government/ its authorized agency to intercept, monitor or decrypt any information generated, transmitted, received or stored in any computer resource if it is necessary or expedient so to do in the interest of the sovereignty or integrity of India, defence of India, security of the State, friendly relations with foreign States or public order or for preventing incitement to the commission of any cognizable offence or for investigation of any offence."</p>
<p>India is setting up a national intelligence grid called <a href="/wiki/NATGRID" title="NATGRID">NATGRID</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> which would be fully set up by May 2011 where each individual's data ranging from land records, Internet logs, air and rail PNR, phone records, gun records, driving license, property records, insurance, and income tax records would be available in real time and with no oversight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> With a UID from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unique_Identification_Authority_of_India" title="Unique Identification Authority of India">Unique Identification Authority of India</a> being given to every Indian from February 2011, the government would be able track people in real time. A national population registry of all citizens will be established by the 2011 census, during which fingerprints and iris scans would be taken along with GPS records of each household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>As per the initial plan, access to the combined data will be given to 11 agencies, including the <a href="/wiki/Research_and_Analysis_Wing" title="Research and Analysis Wing">Research and Analysis Wing</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_Bureau_(India)" title="Intelligence Bureau (India)">Intelligence Bureau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Directorate" title="Enforcement Directorate">Enforcement Directorate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Investigation_Agency" title="National Investigation Agency">National Investigation Agency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Central Bureau of Investigation">Central Bureau of Investigation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Directorate_of_Revenue_Intelligence" title="Directorate of Revenue Intelligence">Directorate of Revenue Intelligence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Narcotics_Control_Bureau" title="Narcotics Control Bureau">Narcotics Control Bureau</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran">Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> is one of the five countries on Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in naturally active efforts to news providers . The government runs or controls almost all of the country’s institutions for regulating, managing or legislating on telecommunications. The Supreme Council for Cyberspace, which was headed by <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Ahmadinejad" title="Mahmoud Ahmadinejad">President Ahmadinejad</a>, was established in March 2012 and now determines digital policy. The construction of a parallel "Iranian Internet", with a high connection speed but fully monitored and censored, is almost complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tools used by the Iranian authorities to monitor and control the Internet include data interception tools capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Packet_Inspection" title="Deep Packet Inspection">Deep Packet Inspection</a>. Interception products from leading Chinese companies such as <a href="/wiki/ZTE" title="ZTE">ZTE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huawei" title="Huawei">Huawei</a> are in use. The products provided by Huawei to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobin_Net" title="Mobin Net">Mobin Net</a>, the leading national provider of mobile broadband, can be used to analyze email content, track browsing history and block access to sites. The products that ZTA sold to the <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication_Company_of_Iran" title="Telecommunication Company of Iran">Telecommunication Company of Iran</a> (TCI) offer similar services plus the possibility of monitoring the mobile network. European companies are the source of other spying and data analysis tools. Products designed by <a href="/wiki/Ericsson" title="Ericsson">Ericsson</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nokia_Solutions_and_Networks" title="Nokia Solutions and Networks">Nokia Siemens Networks</a> (later Trovicor) are in use. These companies sold <a href="/wiki/Short_Message_Service" title="Short Message Service">SMS</a> interception and user location products to Mobile Communication Company of Iran and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irancell" title="Irancell">Irancell</a>, Iran’s two biggest mobile phone companies, in 2009 and they were used to identify Iranian citizens during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2009%E2%80%9310_Iranian_election_protests" title="2009–10 Iranian election protests">post-election uprising</a> in 2009. The use of Israeli surveillance devices has also been detected in Iran. The network traffic management and surveillance device NetEnforcer was provided by Israel to Denmark and then resold to Iran. Similarly, US equipment has found its way to Iran via the Chinese company ZTE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to a 2004 report, the government of the Netherlands carries out more clandestine wire-taps and intercepts than any country, per capita, in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Italy_and_the_Netherlands_top_wiretaps_chart._EDRi-gram_15_July_2004_from_EDRI.org_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Italy_and_the_Netherlands_top_wiretaps_chart._EDRi-gram_15_July_2004_from_EDRI.org-40">[40]</a></sup> The Dutch military intelligence service MIVD operates a satellite ground station to intercept foreign satellite links and also a facility to eavesdrop on foreign high-frequency radio traffic.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_North_Korea" title="Mass surveillance in North Korea">Mass surveillance in North Korea</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/SORM" title="SORM">SORM</a> (and SORM-2) laws enable complete monitoring of any <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">electronic</a> or traditional, by eight state agencies, without warrant. These laws seem to be in conflict with Article 23 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Russia" title="Constitution of Russia">Constitution of Russia</a> which states:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1993const_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-1993const-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<ol>
<li>Everyone shall have the right to the inviolability of private life, personal and family secrets, the protection of honour and good name.</li>
<li>Everyone shall have the right to privacy of correspondence, of telephone conversations, postal, telegraph and other messages. Limitations of this right shall be allowed only by court decision.</li>
</ol>
</blockquote>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court for Human Rights</a> ruled that the legislation violated <a href="/wiki/Article_8_of_the_European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="Article 8 of the European Convention on Human Rights">Article 8 of the European Convention on Human Rights</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Zakharov_v._Russia" title="Zakharov v. Russia">Zakharov v. Russia</a></i>).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Yarovaya_Law" title="Yarovaya Law">Yarovaya Law</a> required storage and unconditional access to private communication data for law enforcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to 2009, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Defence_Radio_Establishment_(Sweden)" title="National Defence Radio Establishment (Sweden)">National Defence Radio Establishment</a> (FRA) was limited to wireless <a href="/wiki/Signals_intelligence" title="Signals intelligence">signals intelligence</a> (SIGINT), although it was left largely unregulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In December 2009, new legislation went into effect, allowing the FRA to monitor cable bound signals passing the Swedish border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turerna_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-turerna-44">[44]</a></sup> Communications service providers are legally required, under confidentiality, to transfer cable communications crossing Swedish borders to specific "interaction points", where data may be accessed after a court order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The number of companies affected by the legislation was estimated as "being limited (approximately ten)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FRA has been contested since the change in its legislation, mainly because of the public perception the change would enable mass surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The FRA categorically deny this allegation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turerna_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-turerna-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> as they are not allowed to initialize any surveillance on their own,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-datainspektionen_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-datainspektionen-51">[51]</a></sup> and has no direct access to communication lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> All SIGINT has to be authorized by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Defence_Intelligence_Court_(Sweden)" title="The Defence Intelligence Court (Sweden)">special court</a> and meet a set of narrow requirements, something <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Defence_(Sweden)" title="Minister for Defence (Sweden)">Minister for Defence</a> <a href="/wiki/Sten_Tolgfors" title="Sten Tolgfors">Sten Tolgfors</a> have been quoted as saying, "should render the debate on mass surveillance invalid."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-limits_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-limits-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villkor_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-villkor-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Due to the architecture of Internet backbones in the Nordic area, a large portion of Norwegian and Finnish traffic will also be affected by the Swedish wiretapping.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syria">Syria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Syria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> is one of the five countries on Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in active, intrusive surveillance of news providers, resulting in grave violations of freedom of information and human rights. Syria has stepped up its web censorship and cyber-monitoring as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" title="Syrian civil war">country’s civil war</a> has intensified. At least 13 <a href="/wiki/Blue_Coat_Systems" title="Blue Coat Systems">Blue Coat proxy servers</a> are in use, <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">Skype</a> calls are intercepted, and <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(security)" title="Social engineering (security)">social engineering techniques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phishing" title="Phishing">phishing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a> attacks are all in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Mass surveillance in the United Kingdom">Mass surveillance in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>State surveillance in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> has formed part of the public consciousness since the 19th century. The postal espionage crisis of 1844 sparked the first panic over the privacy of citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, in the 20th century, electronic surveillance capabilities grew out of wartime <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_intelligence" title="Signal intelligence">signal intelligence</a> and pioneering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_breaking" title="Code breaking">code breaking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1946, the <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" title="Government Communications Headquarters">Government Communications Headquarters</a> (GCHQ) was formed. The United Kingdom and the United States signed the bilateral <a href="/wiki/UKUSA_Agreement" title="UKUSA Agreement">UKUSA Agreement</a> in 1948. It was later broadened to include Canada, Australia and New Zealand, as well as cooperation with several "third-party" nations. This became the cornerstone of Western intelligence gathering and the "<a href="/wiki/Special_Relationship" title="Special Relationship">Special Relationship</a>" between the UK and the USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timecoldwar_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-timecoldwar-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the growth of the Internet and development of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a>, a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_Global_surveillance_disclosure" title="2013 Global surveillance disclosure">media reports in 2013</a> revealed more recent programs and techniques involving GCHQ, such as <a href="/wiki/Tempora" title="Tempora">Tempora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of these capabilities is controlled by laws made in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">UK Parliament</a>. In particular, access to the content of private messages (that is, interception of a communication) must be authorized by a warrant signed by a <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_(United_Kingdom)" title="Secretary of State (United Kingdom)">Secretary of State</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In addition European Union <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Directive" title="Data Protection Directive">data privacy</a> law applies in UK law. The UK exhibits governance and safeguards as well as use of electronic surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Investigatory_Powers_Tribunal" title="Investigatory Powers Tribunal">Investigatory Powers Tribunal</a>, a judicial oversight body for the intelligence agencies, ruled in December 2014 that the legislative framework in the United Kingdom does not breach the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20141206_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20141206-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPT20141205_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPT20141205-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refIPTList_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-refIPTList-68">[68]</a></sup> However, the Tribunal stated in February 2015 that one particular aspect, the data-sharing arrangement that allowed UK Intelligence services to request data from the US surveillance programs <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">Prism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upstream_collection" title="Upstream collection">Upstream</a>, had been in contravention of human rights law prior to this until two paragraphs of additional information, providing details about the procedures and safeguards, were disclosed to the public in December 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In its December 2014 ruling, the Investigatory Powers Tribunal found that the legislative framework in the United Kingdom does not permit mass surveillance and that while GCHQ collects and analyses data in bulk, it does not practice mass surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC20141206_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20141206-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPT20141205_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPT20141205-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refIPTList_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-refIPTList-68">[68]</a></sup> A report on Privacy and Security published by the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_and_Security_Committee_of_Parliament" title="Intelligence and Security Committee of Parliament">Intelligence and Security Committee of Parliament</a> also came to this view, although it found past shortcomings in oversight and said the legal framework should be simplified to improve transparency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISC2015March_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISC2015March-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> This view is supported by independent reports from the <a href="/wiki/Interception_of_Communications_Commissioner" title="Interception of Communications Commissioner">Interception of Communications Commissioner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> However, notable civil liberties groups continue to express strong views to the contrary and plan to appeal the ruling to the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> while others have criticised these viewpoints in turn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refTimes20150313_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-refTimes20150313-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg/220px-Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg/330px-Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg/440px-Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menwith-hill-radomes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/RAF_Menwith_Hill" title="RAF Menwith Hill">RAF Menwith Hill</a>, a large site in the United Kingdom, part of <a href="/wiki/ECHELON" title="ECHELON">ECHELON</a> and the <a href="/wiki/UKUSA_Agreement" title="UKUSA Agreement">UKUSA Agreement</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_Investigatory_Powers_Act_2000" title="Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act 2000">Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act 2000</a> (RIP or RIPA) is a significant piece of legislation that granted and regulated the powers of public bodies to carry out surveillance and investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIPA2000_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-RIPA2000-78">[78]</a></sup> In 2002 the UK government announced plans to extend the Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act so that at least 28 government departments would be given powers to access <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> about citizens' web, e-mail, telephone and fax records, without a warrant and without a subject's knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Protection_of_Freedoms_Act_2012" title="Protection of Freedoms Act 2012">Protection of Freedoms Act 2012</a> includes several provisions related to controlling and restricting the collection, storage, retention, and use of information in government databases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-POFAct2012_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-POFAct2012-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supported by all three major political parties, the UK Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Data_Retention_and_Investigatory_Powers_Act_2014" title="Data Retention and Investigatory Powers Act 2014">Data Retention and Investigatory Powers Act</a> in July 2014 to ensure police and security services retain existing powers to access phone and Internet records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investigatory_Powers_Bill" title="Investigatory Powers Bill">Investigatory Powers Bill</a> published in November 2015 for scrutiny by Parliament would require Internet service providers and mobile phone companies to maintain records of (but not the content of) customers' Internet connections for 12 months. In addition, it would create a requirement for judges to review the warrants authorised by a Secretary of State before they come into force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many advanced nation-states have implemented laws that partially protect citizens from unwarranted intrusion, such as the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act 1998</a> and <a href="/wiki/Data_Protection_Act_1998" title="Data Protection Act 1998">Data Protection Act 1998</a> in the United Kingdom, and laws that require a formal warrant before private data may be gathered by a government.</p>
<p>The UK is a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, participates in its programs, and is subject to <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance#European_Union" title="Mass surveillance">EU policies and directives on surveillance</a>.</p>
<p>The vast majority of video surveillance cameras in the UK are not operated by government bodies, but by private individuals or companies, especially to monitor the interiors of shops and businesses. According to 2011 <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_2000" title="Freedom of Information Act 2000">Freedom of Information Act</a> requests, the total number of local government operated CCTV cameras was around 52,000 over the entirety of the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The prevalence of video surveillance in the UK is often overstated due to unreliable estimates being requoted;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> for example one report in 2002 extrapolated from a very small sample to estimate the number of cameras in the UK at 4.2 million (of which 500,000 in London).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> More reliable estimates put the number of private and local government operated cameras in the United Kingdom at around 1.85 million in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_in_the_United_States" title="Mass surveillance in the United States">Mass surveillance in the United States</a></div>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:NSA_surveillance" title="Template:NSA surveillance"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:NSA_surveillance" title="Template talk:NSA surveillance"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:NSA_surveillance&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Historically, mass surveillance was used as part of wartime censorship to control communications that could damage the war effort and aid the enemy. For example, during the world wars, every international telegram from or to the United States sent through companies such as <a href="/wiki/Western_Union" title="Western Union">Western Union</a> was reviewed by the US military. After the wars were over, surveillance continued in programs such as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Chamber" title="Black Chamber">Black Chamber</a> following World War I and <a href="/wiki/Project_SHAMROCK" title="Project SHAMROCK">project Shamrock</a> following World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/COINTELPRO" title="COINTELPRO">COINTELPRO</a> projects conducted by the U.S. Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) between 1956 and 1971 targeted various "subversive" organizations, including peaceful anti-war and racial equality activists such as <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr" title="Martin Luther King Jr">Martin Luther King Jr</a>.</p>
<p>Billions of dollars per year are spent, by agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA) and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI), to develop, purchase, implement, and operate systems such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivore_(FBI)" title="Carnivore (FBI)">Carnivore</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echelon_(signals_intelligence)" title="Echelon (signals intelligence)">ECHELON</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NarusInsight" title="NarusInsight">NarusInsight</a> to intercept and analyze the immense amount of data that traverses the Internet and telephone system every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zdnet-fbi_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-zdnet-fbi-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001,_terrorist_attacks" title="September 11, 2001, terrorist attacks">September 11, 2001, terrorist attacks</a>, a vast domestic intelligence apparatus has been built to collect information using the NSA, FBI, local police, state homeland security offices and military criminal investigators. The intelligence apparatus collects, analyzes and stores information about millions of (if not all) American citizens, many of whom have not been accused of any wrongdoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo2_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo2-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Mail_Isolation_Control_and_Tracking" title="Mail Isolation Control and Tracking">Mail Isolation Control and Tracking</a> program, the U.S. Postal Service photographs the exterior of every piece of paper mail that is processed in the United States — about 160 billion pieces in 2012. The U.S. Postmaster General stated that the system is primarily used for mail sorting, but the images are available for possible use by law enforcement agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APInterview-August2013_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-APInterview-August2013-94">[94]</a></sup> Created in 2001 following the anthrax attacks that killed five people, it is a sweeping expansion of a 100-year-old program called "<a href="/wiki/Mail_cover" title="Mail cover">mail cover</a>" which targets people suspected of crimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USMailLogging-NYT-July2013_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-USMailLogging-NYT-July2013-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FBI developed the computer programs "<a href="/wiki/Magic_Lantern_(software)" title="Magic Lantern (software)">Magic Lantern</a>" and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIPAV" title="CIPAV">CIPAV</a>, which they can remotely install on a computer system, in order to monitor a person's computer activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teen-busted-in-wired_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-teen-busted-in-wired-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NSA has been gathering information on financial records, Internet surfing habits, and monitoring e-mails. They have also performed extensive analysis of <a href="/wiki/Social_network_analysis" title="Social network analysis">social networks</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Myspace" title="Myspace">Myspace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM</a> special source operation system legally immunized private companies that cooperate voluntarily with U.S. intelligence collection. According to <i>The Register</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FISA_Amendments_Act_of_2008" title="FISA Amendments Act of 2008">FISA Amendments Act of 2008</a> "specifically authorizes intelligence agencies to monitor the phone, email, and other communications of U.S. citizens for up to a week without obtaining a warrant" when one of the parties is outside the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian6_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian6-98">[98]</a></sup> PRISM was first publicly revealed on 6 June 2013, after classified documents about the program were leaked to <i>The Washington Post</i> and <i>The Guardian</i> by American <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Communications_Assistance_for_Law_Enforcement_Act" title="Communications Assistance for Law Enforcement Act">Communications Assistance for Law Enforcement Act</a> (CALEA) requires that all U.S. telecommunications and Internet service providers modify their networks to allow easy <a href="/wiki/Telephone_tapping" title="Telephone tapping">wiretapping</a> of telephone, VoIP, and broadband Internet traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eff-calea-archive_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-eff-calea-archive-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eff-calea-summary_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-eff-calea-summary-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eff-calea-faq_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-eff-calea-faq-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> reported that several major telephone companies were providing the telephone call records of U.S. citizens to the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA), which is storing them in a large database known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSA_call_database" title="NSA call database">NSA call database</a>. This report came on the heels of allegations that the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">U.S. government</a> had been conducting electronic surveillance of domestic telephone calls without warrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In 2013, the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Hemisphere_Project" title="Hemisphere Project">Hemisphere Project</a>, through which AT&amp;T provides telephone call data to federal agencies, became publicly known.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Road-rule_enforcement_camera" title="Road-rule enforcement camera">Traffic cameras</a>, which were meant to help enforce traffic laws at intersections, may be used by law enforcement agencies for purposes unrelated to traffic violations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Some cameras allow for the identification of individuals inside a vehicle and license plate data to be collected and time stamped for cross reference with other data used by police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="United States Department of Homeland Security">Department of Homeland Security</a> is funding networks of surveillance cameras in cities and towns as part of its efforts to combat terrorism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police_Department" title="New York City Police Department">New York City Police Department</a> infiltrated and compiled dossiers on protest groups before the <a href="/wiki/2004_Republican_National_Convention" title="2004 Republican National Convention">2004 Republican National Convention</a>, leading to over 1,800 arrests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> is one of the five countries on Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 list of "State Enemies of the Internet", countries whose governments are involved in active, intrusive surveillance of news providers, resulting in grave violations of freedom of information and human rights. Most of the country’s 16 service providers are directly or indirectly controlled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vietnamese_Communist_Party" title="Vietnamese Communist Party">Vietnamese Communist Party</a>. The industry leader, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_Posts_and_Telecommunications_Group" title="Vietnam Posts and Telecommunications Group">Vietnam Posts and Telecommunications Group</a>, which controls 74 per cent of the market, is state-owned. So is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viettel_Mobile" title="Viettel Mobile">Viettel</a>, an enterprise of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(Vietnam)" title="Ministry of Defence (Vietnam)">Vietnamese armed forces</a>. FPT Telecom is a private firm, but is accountable to the Party and depends on the market leaders for bandwidth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-9"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Service providers are the major instruments of control and surveillance. Bloggers monitored by the government frequently undergo <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a>. These are designed to intercept data meant to be sent to secure (https) sites, allowing passwords and other communication to be intercepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup> According to a July 2012 <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> report, 91 percent of survey respondents connected to the Internet on their mobile devices and the government monitors conversations and tracks the calls of "activists" or "reactionaries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_mass_surveillance">Commercial mass surveillance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Commercial mass surveillance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mass_surveillance_industry" title="Mass surveillance industry">Mass surveillance industry</a></div>
<p>As a result of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_revolution" title="Digital revolution">digital revolution</a>, many aspects of life are now captured and stored in digital form. Concern has been expressed that governments may use this information to conduct mass surveillance on their populations. Commercial mass surveillance often makes use of <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a> laws and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clickwrap" title="Clickwrap">user agreements</a>" to obtain (typically uninformed) 'consent' to surveillance from consumers who use their software or other related materials. This allows gathering of information which would be technically illegal if performed by government agencies. This data is then often shared with government agencies - thereby - in practice - defeating the purpose of such privacy protections.</p>
<p>One of the most common forms of mass surveillance is carried out by commercial organizations. Many people are willing to join supermarket and grocery <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loyalty_card" title="Loyalty card">loyalty card</a> programs, trading their personal information and surveillance of their shopping habits in exchange for a discount on their groceries, although base prices might be increased to encourage participation in the program. Since a significant proportion of purchases are carried out by credit or debit cards, which can also be easily tracked, it is questionable whether loyalty cards provide any significant additional privacy threat.</p>
<p>Through programs like <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s <a href="/wiki/AdSense" title="AdSense">AdSense</a>, <a href="/wiki/OpenSocial" title="OpenSocial">OpenSocial</a> and their increasing pool of so-called "web gadgets", "social gadgets" and other Google-hosted services many web sites on the Internet are effectively feeding user information about sites visited by the users, and now also their social connections, to Google. <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> also keep this information, although its acquisition is limited to page views within Facebook. This data is valuable for authorities, advertisers and others interested in profiling users, trends and web site marketing performance. Google, Facebook and others are increasingly becoming more guarded about this data as their reach increases and the data becomes more all inclusive, making it more valuable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>New features like <a href="/wiki/Geolocation" title="Geolocation">geolocation</a> give an even increased admission of monitoring capabilities to large service providers like Google, where they also are enable to track one's physical movements while users are using mobile devices, especially those which are syncing without any user interaction. Google's <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a> service is increasingly employing features to work as a stand-alone application which also might activate while a web browser is not even active for synchronizing; a feature mentioned on the <a href="/wiki/Google_I/O" title="Google I/O">Google I/O</a> 2009 developer conference while showing the upcoming <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> features which Google and others are actively defining and promoting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008 at the <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Davos" title="Davos">Davos</a>, Google CEO Eric Schmidt, said: "The arrival of a truly mobile Web, offering a new generation of location-based advertising, is set to unleash a 'huge revolution'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> At the Mobile World Congress in Barcelona on 16 February 2010, Google presented their vision of a new business model for mobile operators and trying to convince mobile operators to embrace <a href="/wiki/Location-based_service" title="Location-based service">location-based services</a> and advertising. With Google as the advertising provider, it would mean that every mobile operator using their location-based advertising service would be revealing the location of their mobile customers to Google.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Google will also know more about the customer - because it benefits the customer to tell Google more about them. The more we know about the customer, the better the quality of searches, the better the quality of the apps. The operator one is "required", if you will, and the Google one will be optional. And today I would say, a minority choose to do that, but I think over time a majority will... because of the stored values in the servers and so forth and so on....</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— 2010 Mobile World Congress keynote speech, Google CEO Eric Schmidt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Organizations like the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> are constantly informing users on the importance of privacy, and considerations about technologies like geolocation.</p>
<p>Computer company <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> patented in 2011 a product distribution system with a camera or capture device that monitors the viewers that consume the product, allowing the provider to take "remedial action" if the actual viewers do not match the distribution license.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reporters Without Borders' March 2013 <i>Special report on Internet Surveillance</i> contained a list of "Corporate Enemies of the Internet", companies that sell products that are liable to be used by governments to violate human rights and freedom of information. The five companies on the initial list were: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amesys" title="Amesys">Amesys</a> (France), <a href="/wiki/Blue_Coat_Systems" title="Blue Coat Systems">Blue Coat Systems</a> (U.S.), Gamma (UK and Germany), Hacking Team (Italy), and Trovicor (Germany), but the list was not exhaustive and is likely to be expanded in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RSFSRS-March2013_12-11"><a href="#cite_note-RSFSRS-March2013-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveillance_state">Surveillance state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Surveillance state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A surveillance state is a country where the government engages in pervasive surveillance of large numbers of its citizens and visitors. Such widespread surveillance is usually justified as being necessary to prevent crime or acts of terrorism, but may also be used to stifle criticism of and opposition to the government.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg/230px-DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg/345px-DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg/460px-DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DigiGes_PRISM_Yes_we_scan_-_Demo_am_Checkpoint_Charlie_June_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Germans protesting against the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> surveillance program <a href="/wiki/PRISM_(surveillance_program)" title="PRISM (surveillance program)">PRISM</a> at <a href="/wiki/Checkpoint_Charlie" title="Checkpoint Charlie">Checkpoint Charlie</a> in Berlin.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Examples of early surveillance states include the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, which had a large network of informers and an advanced technology base in computing and spy-camera technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> But these states did not have today's technologies for mass surveillance, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">databases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">pattern recognition</a> software to cross-correlate information obtained by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wire_tapping" title="Wire tapping">wire tapping</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Speech_recognition" title="Speech recognition">speech recognition</a> and telecommunications <a href="/wiki/Traffic_analysis" title="Traffic analysis">traffic analysis</a>, monitoring of financial transactions, <a href="/wiki/Automatic_number_plate_recognition" title="Automatic number plate recognition">automatic number plate recognition</a>, the tracking of the position of mobile telephones, and <a href="/wiki/Facial_recognition_system" title="Facial recognition system">facial recognition systems</a> and the like which recognize people by their appearance, <a href="/wiki/Gait_(human)" title="Gait (human)">gait</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_profiling" title="DNA profiling">DNA profiling</a>, etc.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smart_cities">Smart cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Smart cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mass_surveillance&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
